# The Romantasy Worldbuilding Workbook

---

## What This Is

This is a worldbuilding workbook built specifically for romantasy.

In romantasy, the world isn't just backdrop. It's an active force that shapes and pressures the love story. The political marriage that forces enemies together, the magical bond that connects two people against their will, the ancient feud that makes love forbidden — these aren't setting details. They're the engine of your story.

This workbook helps you build worlds that create romantic tension by design.

---

## Who This Is For

Maybe you want to build fantasy worlds that feel real and internally consistent. Maybe you want external obstacles that actually pressure and test relationships. Maybe you're trying to move beyond generic fantasy settings to a world that could only produce your story.

Whether you're writing your first romantasy or your tenth, whether you're a plotter who needs everything mapped before drafting or a pantser who discovers your world as you go — this workbook meets you where you are.

---

## How This Workbook Is Structured

Each section follows a two-part structure.

The first part is the Foundation. This is the worldbuilding itself — how your geography, magic, government, religion, economy, culture, or history actually works. These elements exist in your world regardless of your characters' romantic situation. Strong foundations make your world feel real. Readers can tell when a world has depth versus when it's cardboard scenery hastily assembled behind the love story.

The second part connects that topic to Romance. This is where worldbuilding becomes romantic tension. Each section shows you how to mine its topic for obstacles, intimacy, stakes, and conflict specific to love stories.

A magic system isn't just about what spells exist. It's about what happens when one character is vastly more powerful than the other, or when a magical bond forces intimacy neither chose, or when using power for someone you love costs something real.

You need both parts. A romance between a servant and a prince needs a believable class system (foundation) that actually creates stakes and obstacles for their relationship (romance application). Skip the foundation and your world feels thin. Skip the romance application and you're not using your world to its full potential.

---

## How to Use This Workbook

You don't have to do everything.

This workbook is comprehensive — intentionally so. Not every section will apply to your story. Not every question will be relevant. If your story doesn't involve inter-species romance, skip that section. If religion isn't a factor, move past it. If your characters aren't part of an institution, those questions aren't for you.

The goal isn't to fill every blank. The goal is to build what your specific story needs.

You don't have to go in order, either. The sections are numbered for reference, not because you must complete them sequentially. Some writers start with magic because that's the heart of their world. Others start with the political situation that creates their central conflict. Some build geography first because place shapes everything else.

Start wherever you have the most energy or the clearest vision. Let that section inform the others.

As you work through each section, keep asking yourself: how does this create obstacles for my characters? Not every piece of worldbuilding needs to directly pressure the romance — some details just make the world feel real. But if you find yourself developing elaborate systems that never touch your love story, you might be worldbuilding for worldbuilding's sake rather than story's sake.

The best romantasy worldbuilding does double duty. It makes the world feel real and it makes the romance feel impossible (until it isn't).

At the end of each section, you'll find Integration Checks — questions that connect that section's topic to others. These are important. Worlds feel real when their systems interact. A magic system affects government (who has power). Religion shapes culture (what's taboo). History explains current politics (why the feud exists). Geography determines economy (who controls resources).

If each section exists in isolation, your world will feel like a collection of unrelated details. Integration Checks help you weave everything together.

Throughout this workbook, you'll find examples drawn from popular romantasy novels — ACOTAR, Fourth Wing, From Blood and Ash, Serpent & Dove, and others. These show what good answers look like and what level of specificity is useful. But these examples aren't templates to copy. Your world should be yours. Use the examples to understand the kind of thinking these questions invite, then develop answers that serve your unique story.

---

## The Sections

Here's what you'll find in this workbook.

Section 1 covers Geography and Environment — the physical world, climate, travel, and how place creates obstacles. Section 2 explores Flora, Fauna, and Ecosystems — plants, animals, bonded creatures, and inter-species romance. Section 3 dives into Culture, Customs, and Daily Life — social norms, taboos, courtship, and what's forbidden.

Section 4 handles Economy, Labor, and Resources — wealth, class, debt, and what money means for love. Section 5 tackles Government, Power, and Law — politics, law, forbidden relationships, and enemies-to-lovers dynamics. Section 6 addresses Religion, Myth, and Prophecy — faith, vows, divine purpose, and what the gods demand.

Section 7 explores History and Lore — the past that haunts the present and inherited conflict. Section 8 develops your Magic System — how magic works, magical bonds, and power dynamics.

Sections 9 through 21 cover specialized topics: technology, time and calendar, language, magic conflicts, pressure cooker settings, institutions, and more.

Appendix A offers a practical guide to revealing worldbuilding in prose without info-dumps.

---

## A Note on Tropes

Romantasy is a trope-heavy genre, and that's not a weakness — it's a feature.

Readers come to romantasy wanting enemies-to-lovers, forced proximity, forbidden love, fated mates, morally grey love interests, and "there's only one bed." This workbook doesn't shy away from tropes. Instead, it helps you execute them well.

The difference between a tired trope and a beloved one isn't the trope itself — it's the execution. An enemies-to-lovers romance where the enmity is shallow and easily overcome is boring. An enemies-to-lovers romance where both characters have genuine, legitimate reasons to hate each other — where choosing love means betraying everything they believed — that's compelling.

Worldbuilding is how you make tropes feel earned.

The forced proximity isn't just "they're stuck together." It's the specific institution, geography, political situation, or magical bond that makes escape impossible. The forbidden love isn't just "they're not supposed to be together." It's the specific laws, cultural taboos, religious strictures, or historical enmity that makes their love dangerous.

Build your world to support your tropes, and your tropes will sing.

---

## Before You Begin

You don't need to know your whole story before starting.

Many writers discover their plots through worldbuilding — realizing that the magic system they developed creates a perfect obstacle, or that the political situation they built suggests an ending they hadn't considered. Let the worldbuilding work with your story, not as a separate task you complete before writing.

Remember: worldbuilding exists to serve the story. A beautifully detailed world that doesn't create stakes for your characters is just a setting guide. A sparse world that perfectly pressures your romance is doing its job.

Build what you need. Skip what you don't. Make a world worth falling in love in.

---

## Let's Begin

Turn to whichever section calls to you first. There's no wrong starting point.

Your world is waiting to be built.


---


# SECTION 1: Geography & Environment

Geography isn't just a map. It's the physical constraints that shape everything else.

Mountains create borders that become political divisions. Deserts isolate cultures. Rivers determine where cities grow. Control of fertile valleys determines who holds power. The physical world you build will shape where people live, how they travel, and what obstacles stand between your characters.

This section has two parts. Part One covers the Foundation — the physical reality of your world. Terrain, climate, resources, dangers, and how geography shapes daily life. Part Two connects Geography to Romance — how the physical world creates romantic tension through separation, proximity, dangerous journeys, and places that hold emotional meaning.

You need both. A romance separated by mountain ranges needs believable geography. A world with interesting terrain needs to show how that geography actually affects your love story.

---

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question here will apply to your story. Some worlds don't have displaced peoples. Some romances don't involve travel. Some settings don't have magical geography.

Skip what doesn't fit. If a section isn't relevant to your world, move on.

Maybe you want to develop your climate system in detail even though it won't directly impact your romance plot. That's fine — a richly developed world has value beyond what appears on the page. Use the romance connection prompts for brainstorming if they're helpful, or leave them blank if that element is just background texture.

If you're not sure whether something matters yet, skip it and come back once your story has taken more shape.

This workbook is a tool, not a test. Use what helps.

---

## How to Think About Geography in Romantasy

Geography in romantasy functions as a pressure system. The physical world can compress your characters together, force them apart, or create conditions where they must rely on each other.

Before you draw a map, ask yourself: what geographic situation does my romance need?

Not every hill and river needs to serve the plot. But the geography you emphasize — the locations where scenes happen, the terrain your characters traverse, the distances between them — should be doing something. Background geography creates atmosphere and believability. Foreground geography creates story.

When you're deciding which geographic elements to develop in detail, consider whether they create friction. Friction can exist between your characters, or between what they want and what they can have.

Separation friction is about what keeps them apart. Maybe different kingdoms are separated by mountains. Maybe an ocean takes months to cross. Maybe a magical barrier can only be crossed under certain conditions, or war zones make travel deadly.

Proximity friction is about what forces them together despite themselves. A snowed-in mountain pass. A ship voyage with no escape. An isolated estate or academy. A dangerous journey that requires partnership.

Resource friction is about what geographic scarcity creates conflict. Water rights in a desert region. The only pass through the mountains. A rare magical resource found in contested territory. Trade routes that require cooperation between enemies.

Geography that doesn't create friction isn't wasted — it's just setting. But when you're choosing what to develop deeply, friction-creating geography gives you the most story material.

The most effective romantasy settings function as pressure cookers. These are environments that trap characters together and force interaction.

Physical trapping includes islands, academies, sieges, ships, and remote estates. Circumstantial trapping covers dangerous wilderness requiring partnership, diplomatic missions, or shared exile. Environmental trapping involves storms, seasons, or magical barriers that prevent departure.

Ask yourself: if my characters could simply walk away from each other, would they? If yes, your geography might need to make walking away impossible, dangerous, or costly.

---

## Common Geographic Patterns in Romantasy

Let's look at the kinds of settings that work especially well for romantasy.

Some settings force proximity. The Academy or Training Ground puts characters in close quarters with no escape — they live, train, and compete together. Think Fourth Wing or Zodiac Academy. The Isolated Estate works similarly with a limited cast, limited space, and limited options for avoidance. Gothic romances and Pride and Prejudice variations use this well.

Ships and caravans are moving containers with enforced proximity and shared danger. Siege and war camp settings use external threat to force cooperation — death is the alternative. Diplomatic missions put enemies together, representing opposing sides. Wilderness journeys require partnership for survival and reveal character through danger.

Geographic barriers create stakes in different ways. Mountain ranges separate nations and cultures, making travel seasonal. Characters might come from opposite sides, or get trapped together for winter. Oceans create complete separation and make departure feel permanent — good for letters across distance and the weight of leaving.

Deserts isolate cultures and make resources precious, opening up oasis politics and survival journeys. Magical borders that only certain people can cross create situations where one character can pass and the other cannot. War zones make travel deadly and create no-man's-land between peoples. Political borders mean crossing is treason or exile — perfect for forbidden love across enemy lines.

Environmental conditions affect romance too. Harsh winters trap characters indoors and require shared warmth. Dangerous nights mean characters must shelter together after dark, creating shared watches and protective sleeping arrangements. Seasonal access — when passes close or ships can't sail — creates racing against time or being trapped until spring.

Resource scarcity makes sharing necessary and generosity meaningful. Magical weather is unpredictable and can trap or separate characters without warning.

---

## Common Mistakes with Geographic Worldbuilding

Let's talk about what goes wrong.

The first mistake is the Teleporting World. Characters travel vast distances with no sense of time passing. A journey that should take weeks happens between chapters with no mention of how they traveled, what they ate, or what dangers they faced. This hurts your romance because distance loses all weight. Separation has no stakes. The reader never feels how far apart your characters are, so reunion carries no emotional punch.

To fix this, establish travel times and stick to them. If crossing the desert takes three weeks, show us at least some of that journey. Use travel time for character development, tension building, or relationship progression. The journey itself can be where the romance happens.

The second mistake is Convenient Geography. Geographic features appear or disappear based on plot needs. A mountain range exists when you need to separate characters but is never mentioned when they need to reunite. A storm strands them together but the weather is otherwise always fine.

Readers notice. If geography is only invoked when convenient, the world feels fake — and stakes that feel fake are stakes that don't matter. Establish your geography early and let it constrain you. If that mountain range exists, it exists whether it's convenient or not. Let the physical world create problems you didn't plan for. Those problems often generate the best scenes.

The third mistake is the Empty Map. You have a map with named places, but none of them feel lived-in. Cities are just labels. Regions have names but no character. The geography exists but doesn't affect anything.

Geography should create culture, which creates characters, which creates conflict. If your geography doesn't explain why people are the way they are, your world is just a painted backdrop. For every major geographic feature, ask yourself: how does this shape the people who live here? Mountain people versus valley people. Desert traders versus forest dwellers. Coastal cultures versus inland cultures. Let geography explain cultural difference, which explains why your characters see the world differently.

The fourth mistake is the Consequence-Free Environment. Your world has dangerous regions, harsh climates, and deadly terrain — but no one ever actually suffers consequences from them. Characters trek through the Deadly Wastes and emerge fine. They climb the Frozen Peaks without frostbite.

If danger is all threat and no delivery, readers stop believing in it. And if readers don't believe the world is dangerous, they don't believe your characters need each other to survive it. Let the environment actually hurt people. Not your protagonists necessarily, but someone. Show us the consequences of the dangerous journey — exhaustion, injury, loss. Make the survival meaningful.

The fifth mistake is Geography Without Emotional Resonance. You describe the setting in detail, but it's all physical — mountains are tall, forests are dense, cities are crowded. None of it connects to how your characters feel.

Setting should be emotional landscape. The place where they first met should feel different than other places. The character's homeland should feel like home — sensory details that mean belonging. Give each major location an emotional quality, and let characters experience that quality differently. The same intimidating castle might feel like prison to one character and sanctuary to another. The same vast desert might represent freedom to one and exile to another.

---

## Reference: Travel Times

Here's a quick reference for how far people can travel in a day. These are approximations, but they'll help you keep your world consistent.

Walking on easy terrain covers about 15-20 miles per day for a healthy adult in good weather. Difficult terrain like mountains, forest, or desert slows that to 8-12 miles. Horseback at a sustainable pace gets you 25-35 miles without exhausting the horse, though hard riding can push 50-60 miles — but you can't maintain that long-term. With fresh horses at relay stations (which requires infrastructure), you can cover 100 miles or more. Sailing ships manage 100-150 miles depending on wind and current. Carriages and wagons need roads and cover 20-30 miles, slower on rough terrain.

## Resources & Territorial Control

Geography determines who has what — and therefore, who has power.

Fertile land, fresh water, mineral deposits, trade routes: these are worth fighting over, worth marrying for, worth dying for. In romantasy, resource control often creates the power imbalances that drive conflict between love interests. One character's family controls the only port; the other's people are landlocked and economically strangled. One nation has the magical ore needed for weapons; another has the farmland needed to feed armies.

These geographic facts become personal when your characters are on opposite sides of them. Resource control also explains arranged marriages, political alliances, and the economic pressures that force characters into impossible choices. When you know who controls what, you understand why certain marriages are strategic, why certain borders are contested, and why your characters might not be free to simply follow their hearts.

What natural resources are geographically concentrated?

| Resource | Where It's Found | Who Controls It |
|----------|------------------|-----------------|
| Fertile farmland | | |
| Fresh water | | |
| Timber/forests | | |
| Mineral deposits | | |
| Coastal access/ports | | |
| Mountain passes | | |
| Magical resources | | |

What geographic features give strategic or economic advantage? The only pass through the mountains? The river that enables trade? The natural harbor? The defensible high ground?

| Feature | Location | Who Controls It | How They Got Control |
|---------|----------|-----------------|----------------------|
| | | | |
| | | | |
| | | | |

What geographic resource creates tension or conflict in your world? Water rights? Arable land? Access to the sea? Control of a trade route?

## The Sensory World

Geography isn't just about what's there. It's about what it feels like to be there.

The sensory details of your setting create the emotional texture of scenes. A first kiss hits differently in a garden heavy with night-blooming jasmine than in a stone corridor that smells of torch smoke. A confession of love carries different weight whispered in a forest where the only sound is wind through pines versus shouted over crashing waves.

Think about what your characters experience physically when they're in the spaces where important moments happen. What do they see, hear, smell, feel on their skin? These details ground your romance in a specific place, making it feel real rather than generic.

Also consider contrast. If your characters come from different environments, their sensory experiences of comfort and home will differ. What feels cozy to one might feel suffocating to another. What feels peaceful to one might feel eerily silent to another.

What does your primary setting look, sound, smell, and feel like?

| Sense | Description |
|-------|-------------|
| What you see | |
| What you hear | |
| What you smell | |
| What the air feels like (temperature, humidity, wind) | |

How does the environment change at different times of day?

| Time | Description |
|------|-------------|
| Morning | |
| Midday | |
| Evening | |
| Night | |

## Climate, Weather & Seasons

Weather creates immediate physical conditions that affect what's possible — travel, agriculture, warfare, daily life.

Beyond logistics, climate shapes the rhythm of life in your world, and that rhythm affects when and how your characters can be together. Seasons can function as deadlines (they must marry before winter closes the pass), as waiting periods (trapped together until spring thaw), or as markers of time passing in a slow-burn romance. Seasonal festivals and traditions create natural occasions for characters to interact — or to be painfully reminded of what they can't have.

Think about how climate affects daily intimacy. In hot climates, people rest during the scorching midday and come alive at night. In cold climates, people cluster around heat sources. In rainy seasons, people are trapped indoors. These patterns determine when characters have time together and what they do with it.

What's the overall climate?

## Weather Events

Weather can serve your romance in unexpected ways.

A storm forces two characters to shelter together. A blizzard delays a departure just long enough. A heat wave makes everyone irritable and strips away social niceties. A fog provides cover for a secret meeting.

Extreme weather also raises stakes. Characters surviving a dangerous storm together, one saving the other from exposure, the vulnerability of being cold or wet or frightened — these moments can accelerate emotional intimacy in ways that calm conditions don't.

What extreme weather occurs in your world? Blizzards? Monsoons? Dust storms? Magical weather?

## Light and Darkness

When the sun rises and sets affects what's possible — travel, work, danger, rest.

Light and darkness also shape romantic opportunity. Darkness provides privacy, cover for secret meetings, the intimacy of being the only two people awake. Firelight and candlelight soften faces and create warmth. Daylight is for public performance; nighttime is when masks come off.

In worlds where darkness brings genuine danger — creatures that hunt at night, magic that activates after sundown, cold that kills — characters may be forced into close quarters after dark. Shared watches, sleeping in proximity for safety, the vulnerability of sleeping near someone you don't fully trust. These are romance accelerants.

Think about what people do with their evenings. If travel stops at dusk, evenings become spaces for conversation, for getting to know each other, for the slow work of building connection.

How many hours of daylight exist in your setting? Does this vary by season? Is there magical darkness or eternal light anywhere?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

When is it safe to travel or work, and when is it dangerous? Are there creatures that hunt at night? Magical dangers after dark? Cold that kills?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

What do people typically do after dark? When travel stops, when work ends — how do people spend evenings?

_______________________________________________________________

## Shelter & Survival

When the environment is hostile, shelter becomes essential.

Shelter is intimacy compressed. When characters must share limited space — a cave, a single room at an inn, a tent — they lose the ability to maintain distance. They see each other in vulnerable moments: sleeping, waking, sick, injured, afraid. They must negotiate shared space, make decisions together, depend on each other.

The quality of shelter matters too. A luxurious inn is a different romantic setting than a damp cave. One offers comfort that might lower defenses; the other offers hardship that bonds through shared suffering. Think about what shelter options exist along your characters' journey and what those options force them to confront.

Scarcity of shelter raises stakes further. If there's only one safe place, and your characters must share it with enemies or strangers, conflict and tension multiply. If there's no shelter at all, survival becomes the priority, and everything else falls away.

What shelter options exist in your world's wilderness?

| Shelter Type | What's Available |
|--------------|------------------|
| Natural (caves, overhangs, dense forest) | |
| Constructed (inns, waypoints, abandoned buildings) | |
| Improvised (what travelers carry or build) | |

What happens when shelter is scarce or contested?

Is there a moment in your story when shelter becomes critical?

| Aspect | Details |
|--------|---------|
| The situation | |
| What shelter they find | |
| What this situation reveals or creates | |

---

## Dangerous Regions

Every world has places that are deadly — and therefore avoided, or crossed only with preparation and company.

Dangerous regions serve romance by raising stakes and forcing partnership. When characters must traverse a deadly wilderness, they depend on each other for survival. The danger strips away social pretense — there's no time for posturing when you're being hunted. Characters reveal their true nature under pressure, for better or worse.

Dangerous regions also function as barriers. Maybe the only route between the characters' homelands runs through territory no one survives alone. Maybe one character is the only guide who knows the safe path. Maybe crossing together is the only option, even for people who hate each other.

Think about what makes your dangerous regions dangerous, and what that means for characters who must enter them. Do they need specific skills, equipment, or magic to survive? Does one character have knowledge or abilities the other lacks? What happens if they get separated?

What regions are dangerous or forbidden?

| Region | What Makes It Dangerous | Who Goes There Anyway (and Why) |
|--------|------------------------|--------------------------------|

| | | |

# PART TWO: GEOGRAPHY & ROMANCE

---

## Home & Belonging

Geography isn't just physical. It's emotional. Where do your characters feel they belong? Where do they feel alien?

Home is one of the most emotionally charged concepts in fiction, and in romance, it often becomes central. Characters may leave home to find love, may be torn between love and home, may find that home becomes wherever the other person is. Or they may discover that their love interest's home will never accept them, that choosing this person means exile from everything familiar.

The sensory details of home matter because they're the details of identity. What someone considers home — the sounds, smells, textures that mean safety and belonging — reveals who they are at their core. When characters share these details with each other, they're sharing something intimate.

Think about what happens when characters lack a sense of home. Someone who has never belonged anywhere brings different needs and fears to a relationship than someone with deep roots. The promise of belonging — or the threat of losing it — becomes a powerful romantic stake.

Where does your protagonist feel at home? The place that shaped them, where they feel most themselves:

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

What does home look, smell, sound like to them? The sensory details that mean home:

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

Where does your love interest feel at home?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

What does home mean to them — sensory details?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

Where does each character feel like an outsider?

| Character | Where They Feel Alien |
|-----------|----------------------|
| Protagonist | |
| Love interest | |

Do they ever discuss or share what home means to them?

---

## Entering Each Other's Worlds

If your characters come from different places, visiting each other's territory is significant.

There's a particular vulnerability in bringing someone into your home territory. You see your world through their eyes, and suddenly you notice things you'd stopped seeing — both the beauty and the flaws. You watch them react and feel pride or shame or defensiveness. Their comfort or discomfort becomes your concern.

For the visitor, entering unfamiliar territory means being the outsider, the one who doesn't know the rules, the one who depends on their partner to navigate. It's a reversal that can shift relationship dynamics. The powerful one becomes uncertain; the certain one becomes the guide.

These visits also deepen understanding. You can't fully know someone until you've seen where they come from. The landscape explains the person — why they flinch at certain things, why they find comfort in others, what shaped their expectations of the world.

Does one character visit the other's home territory?

| Aspect | Details |
|--------|---------|
| Who visits whose world | |

What's the sensory experience of entering unfamiliar territory? What do they notice first? What's strange? What's beautiful? What's uncomfortable?

---

## Geography as Emotional Landscape

The physical world can resonate with or contrast against the emotional journey. This works best when it emerges naturally rather than being engineered.

Setting and emotion are always in conversation, whether you plan it or not. A love scene in a sunlit meadow feels different than one in a crumbling tower. A confession during a storm carries different weight than one in stillness. You don't need to engineer pathetic fallacy, but it's worth being intentional about the emotional texture of your key locations.

Think about how a single location can feel different depending on circumstances. The same palace might feel magical during a ball and suffocating during captivity. The same forest might feel enchanting in daylight and terrifying at night. As your characters' relationship changes, the settings they move through might feel different even when the physical reality is the same.

Think about whether any location becomes symbolically meaningful to your characters specifically — a place they return to, a place where something changed, a place that's theirs.

What's the emotional quality of your primary setting? Does it feel oppressive, expansive, peaceful, threatening, beautiful, decaying?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

Does the environment shift over the course of your story? Do characters move from one type of landscape to another? Does the same place feel different as circumstances change?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

Is there a location that becomes meaningful to your characters specifically? A place they return to, a place where something changed, a place that's theirs:

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

---

## Integration Check

These questions connect geography to other sections of the workbook. Use the ACOTAR examples as a guide for the level of specificity that's useful.

| Question | Answer | Example (ACOTAR) |
|----------|--------|------------------|
| How does geography explain cultural differences (from Section 3)? | | Each Court's territory shapes its people: Spring Court's eternal bloom creates beauty-focused culture; Night Court's mountains and stars foster secretive, cerebral society |
| How does geography create or reinforce power structures (from Section 5)? | | High Lords control their territories absolutely; the Wall creates a hard political boundary between fae and human governance |
| How does geography interact with magic (from Part 1)? | | Each Court's magic is tied to its land — Spring magic is growth and renewal; Night magic is darkness and stars. Power weakens outside one's territory |
| What geographic resource or territory is contested in your world? | | The mortal lands beyond the Wall — Hybern wants to conquer them; some fae want to protect them; humans just want to survive |

---

## Quick Reference: Geographic Stakes

Use this table to summarize the key geographic elements of your story.

| Element | Your World | Example (ACOTAR-style) |
|---------|------------|------------------------|
| Key location 1 | | The Spring Court manor — a grand estate surrounded by gardens, where Feyre is both captive and guest |
| Key location 2 | | The Wall — the magical barrier separating the mortal lands from faerie |
| Key location 3 | | Under the Mountain — Amarantha's dark court, site of climactic confrontation |
| Key location 4 | | The mortal village — Feyre's impoverished home, what she left behind |
| Key location 5 | | The Night Court — a place of stars and darkness, initially frightening, later sanctuary |
| The contested resource or territory | | The seven High Lord territories, each controlling different types of magic and land |
| The barrier separating peoples or characters | | The Wall prevents humans from entering faerie (without consequence) and keeps fae from crossing south |
| The dangerous region | | Under the Mountain — a place of trials, corruption, and enslavement |
| The isolated location | | The Spring Court manor — Feyre is the only human, can't leave, surrounded by fae |
| The significant journey (from → to) | | Mortal lands → Spring Court (captivity) → Under the Mountain (trial) → Night Court (transformation) |
| The place that matters emotionally | | The manor's art gallery — where Feyre finds purpose and connection through painting |

---

## Final Thought

Your map isn't just where things are. It's why characters must make the choices they make.

The mountain range isn't just scenery. It's why two nations developed separately and now distrust each other.

The desert isn't just setting. It's why water is precious and whoever controls the oasis controls the trade route.

The ocean isn't just beautiful. It's why these cultures never met before, and why contact now is both wondrous and dangerous.

Build geography that explains your world and creates the situations your story needs. Make your world a character in the story, not just a backdrop for it.


---


# SECTION 2: Flora, Fauna & Ecosystems

The natural world does more than populate scenery. It feeds your population, clothes them, provides medicine, creates danger, enables trade, and sometimes forges bonds between characters.

This section has two parts. Part One covers the Foundation — how the natural world actually works, what people eat, what animals they rely on, what plants they use, what creatures pose danger. Part Two connects Nature to Romance — creature bonds that parallel romantic bonds, survival situations that force trust, caring for animals together, and inter-species love if one or both characters aren't human.

You need both. A romance involving bonded dragons needs a believable ecosystem. A world with interesting creatures needs to show how those creatures actually affect your love story.

---

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question here will apply to your story. Some worlds don't have magical creatures. Some romances don't involve creature bonds or inter-species dynamics. Some stories stay entirely in cities where the natural world is barely present.

If your world is Earth-like, you don't need to reinvent the wheel. Many romantasy stories are set in worlds that function like ours — or like a historical period of ours — but with magic added. If your world has normal horses, normal wheat, normal wolves, you don't need to fill in pages about them. Focus instead on what's different from the baseline readers will assume: the magical creatures, the plants with supernatural properties, the ways magic has changed the ecosystem.

Skip what doesn't fit. If your story doesn't involve wilderness travel, you probably don't need to detail dangerous creatures. If neither character is non-human, skip the inter-species romance sections entirely.

If you're not sure whether something matters yet, skip it and come back later.

---

## How to Think About Flora & Fauna in Romantasy

The natural world in romantasy typically serves story in three ways: as backdrop (atmosphere and texture), as obstacle (danger, scarcity, survival pressure), or as connector (shared experiences, creature bonds, caring for animals together).

Most writers default to backdrop — describing beautiful forests or dangerous beasts without making them do narrative work. But nature becomes powerful when it creates situations your characters must navigate together.

Nature works as pressure when the natural world compresses your characters together or forces them to depend on each other. Dangerous wilderness that requires partnership to survive. Harsh conditions that demand shared shelter, shared warmth, shared resources. Predators that make traveling alone suicidal. Seasonal pressures that trap them together or create deadlines.

Nature works as revelation when how characters interact with the natural world reveals who they are. How they treat animals — gentle? utilitarian? fearful? cruel? What they know about survival — competent? helpless? surprisingly skilled? Their comfort in wilderness versus civilization. Whether they see nature as resource, threat, or home.

Nature works as connection when shared experiences with nature build relationships. Caring for an injured animal together. Surviving a dangerous encounter. One teaching the other to ride, hunt, forage, or track. Witnessing something beautiful or terrifying together.

When developing your world's flora and fauna, keep asking: how might this create pressure, revelation, or connection for my characters?

---

## Common Patterns: Nature in Romantasy

Let's look at how animals typically serve romance in this genre.

The bonded creature creates a magical bond that parallels the romantic bond. The creatures' relationship reflects or contrasts with the humans'. Think dragons in Fourth Wing, or familiars. The shared rescue has characters saving an animal together, establishing cooperation and revealing character — an injured horse, an orphaned creature. The animal that judges only likes one character at first, gradually accepting the other as trust builds. The teaching moment has one character teaching the other to work with animals, creating intimacy through instruction. The animal witness is the pet or mount present during vulnerable moments, normalizing intimacy.

Plants and environment serve romance differently. The rare ingredient quest sends characters to find a specific plant, creating journey and shared purpose — a cure for poison, a magical component. The poisoning/healing arc has one character poisoned while the other must find the cure or nurse them through, creating vulnerability and care. The foraging scene has characters gathering food together, one teaching the other what's safe — domestic intimacy in wilderness. The meaningful flower is a plant with symbolic meaning given as gift or appearing at a significant moment. The dangerous garden has beautiful but deadly plants creating tension between attraction and danger — often a metaphor for the love interest.

Survival pressure serves romance through the predator attack where characters must fight together or protect each other, revealing combat ability and protective instincts. The scarcity share has limited food or water where one sacrifices for the other or they must share. The environmental trap forces proximity and cooperation through flood, fire, or avalanche. The hunting/providing moment has one character hunting for the other — providing food as care.

---

## Common Mistakes with Flora & Fauna

The first mistake is the Decorative Ecosystem. Your world has beautiful forests and exotic creatures, but they never actually affect anything. Characters travel through wilderness without interacting with it. Animals exist but don't matter. A world where nature is just scenery feels thin. If your characters can ignore the natural world entirely, you're missing opportunities for pressure, revelation, and connection. Make characters interact with nature. They need to eat — where does food come from? They travel through wilderness — what dangers exist? Pick a few plants or animals and make them matter to daily life, economy, or plot.

The second mistake is the Convenient Creature. A magical creature exists solely to bond your characters or give them cool powers. It has no ecology, no behavior patterns, no existence outside its plot function. Creatures that exist only for plot convenience feel engineered. Readers sense when an animal is a device rather than a being. Give creatures lives beyond their plot function. What do they eat? Where do they live? How do they reproduce? What's their relationship with other creatures? Even bonded creatures should have existence and personality independent of their bonded humans.

The third mistake is the Knowledgeless Character. Characters who grew up in a medieval-ish world have no idea how to interact with nature. They can't identify common plants, don't know how to care for horses, are surprised by animal behavior that would be common knowledge. It breaks immersion. People in pre-industrial societies know their environment. A farmer's daughter would know which berries are poisonous. A nobleman would know how to handle horses. Match character knowledge to their background. Rural characters should be competent with nature. Urban characters can be less skilled but shouldn't be completely helpless.

The fourth mistake is the Defanged Wilderness. The wilderness is described as dangerous, but nothing bad ever actually happens. Characters travel through the "deadly forest" without incident. Predators are mentioned but never encountered. Stakes need delivery. If dangerous nature never delivers consequences, readers stop believing in the danger. Let the wilderness be actually dangerous. Not necessarily to protagonists, but someone should face real consequences. Show the aftermath of predator attacks. Let characters get injured, get lost, run out of food. Make survival feel earned.

The fifth mistake is the Bond That Does Everything. A creature bond exists and immediately solves all relationship problems. The dragons chose them, so they're fated! The bond proves they belong together! No further relationship development needed. It shortcuts the actual work of romance. If the bond does the heavy lifting, the characters don't have to. Readers want to see the relationship built, not handed to them by magical creatures. Let the bond create circumstances, not feelings. The bond might force proximity or shared responsibility, but love, trust, and compatibility must still be built by the characters themselves.

---

# PART ONE: THE FOUNDATION

---

## The Ecosystem Foundation

Before you can use nature for story purposes, you need to understand the baseline. What's the general relationship between people and the natural world in your setting?

This foundation shapes everything else. A world where wilderness is mostly tamed has different story possibilities than one where civilization is a fragile island in dangerous nature. A world where magic permeates nature works differently than one where magic and nature are separate.

You don't need extensive ecology — just a clear sense of the human-nature relationship that makes the rest of your worldbuilding coherent.

What's the general relationship between people and nature in your world? Do people dominate their environment? Live in balance? Struggle against nature? Have they tamed the wild or does wilderness still rule?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

How magical is the natural world? Completely mundane (magic is separate from nature)? Subtly magical (nature has hidden power)? Overtly magical (magical creatures are common)? Sentient (nature has awareness or will)?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

What's the overall health of the ecosystem? Thriving? Balanced? Under pressure from civilization? Recovering from past damage? Dying?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

---

## Food Chains & Natural Balance

Understanding what eats what grounds your world in reality. It also determines what's dangerous in the wilderness, what animals people hunt or fear, and how the ecosystem holds together.

You don't need to map every predator-prey relationship, but knowing the top predators and large herbivores in each major terrain gives you the wildlife that characters might actually encounter — or need to avoid.

If your world uses Earth-like wildlife, you can skip most of this — just note any magical creatures or significant deviations that matter to your story.

|-------------|---------------|
| Forests | |
| Plains/grasslands | |
| Mountains | |
| Water (rivers, lakes, ocean) | |

## Domesticated Animals

The animals people have tamed shape daily life, economy, and culture in ways that are easy to overlook. What pulls the plows? What provides meat, milk, leather, wool? What do people ride? These aren't just background details — they determine what's possible in your world.

For romance, domesticated animals often appear in teaching moments (one character showing another how to handle horses), in revelation moments (how someone treats animals shows their character), or as quiet witnesses to intimate scenes. The protagonist's horse has probably seen more of their emotional journey than any human character.

Think about class dimensions too. Who owns livestock versus who tends it? Is riding a noble skill or common knowledge? These details can create or reflect differences between your characters.

If your world uses standard Earth animals (horses, cattle, dogs, etc.), just note what's different or what matters to your story — magical mounts, unusual companions, or specific animals your characters interact with.

What animals have been domesticated in your world?

| Purpose | Animal | Who Keeps Them | Status/Value |
|---------|--------|----------------|--------------|

| Milk/dairy | | | |
| Eggs | | | |
| Wool/fiber | | | |
| Leather/hide | | | |
| Transportation | | | |
| Labor/draft work | | | |
| Hunting/herding | | | |
| Guarding | | | |
| Companionship | | | |

## Agriculture & Cultivated Plants

What grows in your world determines what people eat, wear, and trade. Agriculture also shapes settlement patterns, seasonal rhythms, and the divide between farming and herding cultures.

For romance, agriculture might seem distant, but it affects daily life in ways that touch your characters. Harvest festivals bring people together. Crop failures create desperation. The difference between "wheat country" and "rice country" can mark cultural boundaries. The rhythm of agricultural life — planting, tending, harvesting — creates a calendar that governs when people can travel, when they celebrate, when they have leisure for courtship.

If your world has Earth-like agriculture, skip the basics and focus on what's unique: magical crops, plants with supernatural properties, or specific foods that matter to your story or culture.

What are the staple crops? The grains, roots, or plants that feed most people:

## Wild Plants & Foraging

Beyond cultivation, the wild provides — for those who know how to find it. Foraging knowledge is often the difference between survival and starvation when characters are away from civilization.

In romance, foraging scenes create opportunities for one character to teach another, demonstrating competence and care. Knowing which plants are safe is intimate knowledge, often passed down through families or communities. A character who can keep their partner fed in the wilderness is providing something fundamental.

Poisonous plants also matter — both as genuine danger and as plot device. Knowing what's poisonous is survival knowledge, and that knowledge gap can be deadly.

What can be safely gathered from the wild? Edible plants, nuts, berries, mushrooms:

## Medicine & Healing

How people treat illness and injury affects everything from battlefield survival to childbirth to plague. Medicine also creates story opportunities: the healing scene is a romance staple because it creates vulnerability, physical intimacy, and care.

Think about the gap between mundane and magical healing in your world. If magical healing exists, who has access? What can it do that herbs can't? If it's rare or expensive, most people still rely on traditional medicine — which means knowing herbs and remedies remains valuable knowledge.

If your world uses Earth-like medicine (herbs, poultices, traditional remedies), focus on what's different: magical healing, unique plants with supernatural properties, or the specific mundane-vs-magical divide in your world.

What natural remedies exist? Common herbs for common ailments:

|---------|--------|
| Pain | |
| Fever | |
| Infection/wounds | |
| Illness | |
| Childbirth | |

What's the difference between mundane and magical healing? What can herbs do vs. what requires magic?

## Environmental Challenges

The natural world creates problems beyond predators — disease, weather, scarcity. These challenges shape daily life and can drive plot when characters must deal with environmental crisis.

Environmental threats also create opportunities for characters to show competence, make sacrifices, or work together against a common enemy that isn't human. A plague or famine doesn't care about the political conflict between your characters' peoples — it threatens everyone equally.

What diseases or parasites affect people? Insect-borne illness? Waterborne disease? Parasites from certain regions?

## Extinction & Endangered Species

What's no longer there — or what's disappearing — can matter as much as what exists. Extinct creatures might be the stuff of legend, or their absence might have changed the world. Endangered species might be protected, poached, or ignored.

For story purposes, "the last of its kind" creates automatic stakes. A character who protects an endangered creature, or who is themselves the last of their kind, carries weight that common beings don't.

Are there creatures that used to exist but don't anymore?

## Dangerous Creatures

What makes the wilderness deadly? Dangerous creatures create survival pressure, raise stakes for travel, and provide opportunities for characters to protect each other or demonstrate combat ability.

The best dangerous creatures feel like part of the ecosystem rather than monsters placed to threaten heroes. They have territories, behaviors, reasons for being dangerous. A mother protecting her young is a different encounter than a hungry predator hunting.

What are the most dangerous creatures in your world?

---

## Magical Creatures

Magical creatures occupy a different space than mundane wildlife. They might be rare and legendary, or common as horses. They might be intelligent, tameable, or utterly alien. Their existence changes what's possible in your world.

For romance, magical creatures often serve as externalized metaphors — the dragon's wildness reflecting the love interest's untamed nature, the gentle unicorn revealing the protagonist's hidden purity. This can work beautifully or feel heavy-handed, depending on execution.

More practically, magical creatures create story opportunities through their abilities, their rarity, and their relationship with humans. Can they be bonded? Ridden? Communicated with? These questions open different plot possibilities.

What magical creatures exist in your world?

| Creature | Intelligence | Magical Abilities | Where Found | Relationship with Humans |
|----------|--------------|-------------------|-------------|-------------------------|
| | | | | |
| | | | | |
| | | | | |

How common or rare are magical creatures? Everyday occurrence? Regional? Nearly mythical?

How do magical creatures fit into the ecosystem? Do they fill normal ecological roles? Exist outside natural laws? Disrupt normal nature?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

# PART TWO: NATURE & ROMANCE

---

## Creature Bonds

Some fantasy worlds include creatures that form magical bonds with humans — dragons, familiars, spirit animals. This is common in romantasy because bonded creatures can create or reflect connections between characters.

The creature bond often parallels the romantic bond: two beings learning to trust each other, communicate, work together. When both love interests have bonded creatures, the creatures' relationship can mirror, contrast with, or complicate the humans' relationship.

But creature bonds can also shortcut romantic development if you're not careful. If the dragons chose them for each other, does the relationship feel earned? The best creature bond romances treat the bond as creating circumstances — proximity, shared responsibility, mutual understanding — rather than dictating romantic outcomes.

Do any creatures in your world bond with humans? If no, skip to the next section.

If yes:

Which creatures bond?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

What does the bond cost or demand? Responsibility? Shared pain? Shortened human lifespan? Behavioral changes?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

| Nature of bond | | |

If both characters have bonded creatures, how do the creatures relate to each other?

_______________________________________________________________

_______________________________________________________________

Creature bonds in romantasy can easily become a shortcut — the dragons chose them for each other, so they must be fated. The best versions treat the creature bond as creating circumstances, not dictating romantic outcomes.

If the creatures have some connection, does that force the humans together? Or does it just create proximity, opportunity, or shared responsibility — while the relationship itself must be built by the characters?

## Ordinary Animal Companions

Not every significant animal is a magical bonded creature. Pets, horses, hunting dogs — ordinary animals reveal character and create connection.

How someone treats their horse, their dog, their cat tells you something about them that words can't. The gruff warrior who's gentle with his warhorse. The cold aristocrat whose cat is the only creature she touches with affection. The protagonist who risks everything to save a stray.

Animals also witness relationships without judgment. The horse that carries both characters, the dog that sleeps at the foot of the bed, the cat that accepts the new person in its territory — these small acceptances mark relationship progression.

Does your protagonist have an animal companion?

|--------|---------|

| What the animal means to them | |

|--------|---------|

| Name | |

| What the animal means to them | |

What does how someone treats animals reveal about them? What does your protagonist notice about how the love interest treats animals?

## Working with Animals

Training, riding, hunting, herding — working alongside animals creates its own dynamics. Skill with animals is attractive. Teaching someone to work with animals creates intimacy. Shared work builds partnership.

Do your characters work with animals?

| Aspect | Details |
|--------|---------|
| What kind of work | |
| What skills it requires | |

Is one character more skilled with animals than the other?

| Aspect | Details |
|--------|---------|
| Who, and with what animals | |
| How this affects their dynamic | |

This section bridges into inter-species romance territory. A non-human character's connection to nature — whether they're more animal, more magical, or simply different — shapes how they experience the world and how they relate to human partners.

## Integration Check

These questions connect flora and fauna to other sections of the workbook. Use the Fourth Wing examples as a guide for the level of specificity that's useful.

| Question | Answer | Example (Fourth Wing) |
|----------|--------|----------------------|
| How do plants and animals enable or restrict magic? | | Dragons ARE the source of rider magic — no bond, no power. Fonilee herb masks scent from dragons, affecting who survives selection |
| Does control of creatures or natural resources create political or economic power? | | Navarre's military dominance depends entirely on bonded dragons; nations without them are vulnerable to conquest |
| How have creatures or environmental factors shaped history? (Dragons in wars? Plagues? Famines?) | | The first venin war 400 years ago nearly destroyed everything; dragons and riders are now the only defense |
| What natural resource is contested or controlled in your world? | | Dragons themselves — their fire forges unbreakable weapons; their power decides which nation survives |
